Pakistan's relations with Saudi Arabia has been historically strong, and often Pakistan has feared Iran is trying to recruit its large Shi'a population to serve for Iran's military adventures, given by increasing number of vanishing Shi'as in Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-426"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-426">[426]</a></sup> Its link with Iran is also marred with a number of problems regarding not just Shia issue, but also due to [conflict in Afghanistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan "War in Afghanistan"), with Iran-backed proxies have fought against Pakistan and its ally [Taliban](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taliban "Taliban"), further strengthens Pakistan's relations with Saudi Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-427"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-427">[427]</a></sup> However, Pakistan has refrained from criticizing Iran, but rather seeks to preserve the relations, given its long historical relationship with Iran. Pakistan has backed Iran on its effort to maintain border security in the restive [Balochistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balochistan "Balochistan") region,<sup id="cite_ref-428"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-428">[428]</a></sup> and have cooperated against the Soviets in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-429"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-429">[429]</a></sup>

Pakistan prefers to remain neutral in Saudi-Iran rivalry. It remained neutral during the [2017 Qatar-Gulf crisis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qatar_diplomatic_crisis "Qatar diplomatic crisis").<sup id="cite_ref-Al_Jazeera_Neutral_430-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-Al_Jazeera_Neutral-430">[430]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-431"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-431">[431]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-432"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-432">[432]</a></sup> In 2019, Pakistani prime minister Imran Khan, during a visit to Tehran, said that he was trying promote talks between Iran and Saudi Arabia to defuse the tension between the two countries.<sup id="cite_ref-433"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-433">[433]</a></sup>

## Involvement in regional conflicts [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=43 "Edit section: Involvement in regional conflicts")

### Syrian Civil War [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=44 "Edit section: Syrian Civil War")

[Syria](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syria "Syria") has been a major theater in the proxy conflict throughout its ongoing [civil war](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War "Syrian Civil War"), which began in 2011. Iran and the GCC states have provided varying degrees of military and financial support to opposing sides, with Iran backing the government and Saudi Arabia supporting rebel militants. Syria is an important part of Iran's sphere of influence, and the [government](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Council_of_Ministers_(Syria) "Council of Ministers (Syria)") under [Bashar al-Assad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad "Bashar al-Assad") has long been a major ally. During the early stages of the Arab Spring, [Supreme Leader](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supreme_Leader_of_Iran "Supreme Leader of Iran") [Khamenei](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ali_Khamenei "Ali Khamenei") initially expressed support for the revolutions in [Tunisia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tunisian_Revolution "Tunisian Revolution") and [Egypt](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egyptian_revolution_of_2011 "Egyptian revolution of 2011"), characterizing them as an "Islamic awakening" akin to its own revolution in 1979. When [protests](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civil_uprising_phase_of_the_Syrian_Civil_War "Civil uprising phase of the Syrian Civil War") broke out in Syria, Iran changed its position and condemned them, comparing the uprising to its own [presidential election protests](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2009_Iranian_presidential_election_protests "2009 Iranian presidential election protests") in 2009 and accusing the United States and Israel of being behind the unrest.<sup id="cite_ref-434"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-434">[434]</a></sup>

The war threatens Iran's position, and Saudi Arabia and its allies have sided with Sunni rebels in part to weaken Iran. For years Iranian forces have been involved on the ground, with soldiers in Islamic Revolutionary Guards Corps facing heavy casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-435"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-435">[435]</a></sup> In 2014, with no end in sight to the conflict, Iran increased its ground support for the [Syrian Army](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_Army "Syrian Army"), providing elite forces, intelligence gathering, and training. Iran also backs pro-Assad Hezbollah fighters.<sup id="cite_ref-436"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-436">[436]</a></sup> Although Iran and Saudi Arabia agreed in 2015 to participate in peace talks in Vienna in participation with [United States Secretary of State](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State "United States Secretary of State") [John Kerry](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Kerry "John Kerry") and Russian [Foreign Minister](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_(Russia) "Minister of Foreign Affairs (Russia)") [Sergey Lavrov](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergey_Lavrov "Sergey Lavrov"), the talks ultimately failed.<sup id="cite_ref-437"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-437">[437]</a></sup>

Saudi Arabia countered Russia's intervention in Syria by increasing its support for the rebels and supplying American-made anti-tank [TOW missiles](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/BGM-71_TOW "BGM-71 TOW"), a move which slowed initial progress made by Russian and Syrian forces.<sup id="cite_ref-438"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-438">[438]</a></sup>

### Yemeni Civil War [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=45 "Edit section: Yemeni Civil War")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Air_strike_in_Sana%27a_11-5-2015.jpg)

An airstrike in [Sana'a](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sana%27a "Sana'a") by a Saudi-led coalition on 11 May 2015

Yemen has been called one of the major fronts in the conflict as a result of the [revolution](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yemeni_Revolution "Yemeni Revolution") and subsequent [civil war](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yemeni_Civil_War_(2015%E2%80%93present) "Yemeni Civil War (2015–present)").<sup id="cite_ref-439"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-439">[439]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-440"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-440">[440]</a></sup> Yemen had for years been within the Saudi sphere of influence. The decade-long [Houthi insurgency in Yemen](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Houthi_insurgency_in_Yemen "Houthi insurgency in Yemen") stoked tensions with Iran, with accusations of covert support for the rebels. A 2015 UN report alleged that Iran provided the [Houthi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Houthi "Houthi") rebels with money, training, and arms shipments beginning in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-441"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-441">[441]</a></sup> However, the degree of support has been subject to debate, and accusations of greater involvement have been denied by Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-442"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-442">[442]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-443"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-443">[443]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IranInfo_444-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-IranInfo-444">[444]</a></sup> The [2014–2015 coup d'état](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Houthi_takeover_in_Yemen "Houthi takeover in Yemen") was viewed by Saudi leadership as an immediate threat, and as an opportunity for Iran to gain a foothold in the region. In March 2015, a Saudi-led coalition of Arab states, including all GCC members except [Oman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oman "Oman"), [intervened](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_Arabian-led_intervention_in_Yemen "Saudi Arabian-led intervention in Yemen") and launched airstrikes and a ground offensive in the country, declaring the entire [Saada Governorate](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saada_Governorate "Saada Governorate") a military target and imposing a naval blockade.<sup id="cite_ref-445"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-445">[445]</a></sup>

The United States intervened in October 2016 after missiles were fired at a US warship, which was in place to protect oil shipments along the sea lane passing through the [Mandeb Strait](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb "Bab-el-Mandeb"). The US blamed the rebels and responded by targeting radar sites with missile strikes along the [Red Sea](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_Sea "Red Sea") coast. In response, rebels called the strikes evidence of American support for the Saudi campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-446"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-446">[446]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-447"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-447">[447]</a></sup>

### War in Iraq [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=46 "Edit section: War in Iraq")

While the majority of Muslims in Iraq are Shia, the country has been ruled for decades by Sunni-dominated governments under the [Ottoman Empire](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Empire "Ottoman Empire"), the British-installed [Hashemites](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq "Kingdom of Iraq"), and the [Ba'athists](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ba%27athist_Iraq "Ba'athist Iraq"). Under the rule of Saddam Hussein, Iraq was hostile to both Iran and Saudi Arabia and acted as a counterbalancing regional power. The [American-led invasion](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq "2003 invasion of Iraq") in 2003 caused a power vacuum in the region. With the antagonistic Ba'athist regime removed, Iran sought a more friendly Shia-dominated government and supported sympathetic rebel factions as part of an effort to undermine the coalition, which Iran feared would install a government hostile to its interests.<sup id="cite_ref-448"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-448">[448]</a></sup>

Saudi Arabia remained more passive during the occupation of Iraq, taking caution to preserve its relations with the United States by avoiding any direct support of Sunni insurgent groups. Riyadh supported the [Bush administration](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidency_of_George_W._Bush "Presidency of George W. Bush")'s commitment to stay in the country, as it limited Iran's influence.<sup id="cite_ref-449"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-449">[449]</a></sup> The edicts issued in May 2003 by [Coalition Provisional Authority](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coalition_Provisional_Authority "Coalition Provisional Authority") Administrator [Paul Bremer](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Bremer "Paul Bremer") to exclude members of the [Ba'ath Party](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ba%27ath_Party_(Iraqi-dominated_faction) "Ba'ath Party (Iraqi-dominated faction)") from the new Iraqi government and to disband the Iraqi Army undermined the occupation effort. The orders empowered various insurgent factions and weakened the new government's functional capabilities, leaving Iraq vulnerable to future instability.<sup id="cite_ref-450"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-450">[450]</a></sup>

Following the United States [withdrawal from Iraq](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Withdrawal_of_U.S._troops_from_Iraq_(2007%E2%80%932011) "Withdrawal of U.S. troops from Iraq (2007–2011)") in December 2011, the country drifted further into Iran's sphere of influence. The instability that resulted from the [Iraqi Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraqi_Civil_War_(2014%E2%80%932017) "Iraqi Civil War (2014–2017)") and the rise of ISIL threatened the existence of the Iraqi regime and led to an [Iranian intervention](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iranian_intervention_in_Iraq_(2014%E2%80%93present) "Iranian intervention in Iraq (2014–present)") in 2014. Iran mobilized Shia militia groups to halt and ultimately push back the advancing Sunni insurgency,<sup id="cite_ref-451"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-451">[451]</a></sup> though the resurgence of ISIL in Iraq remains more than a possibility.<sup id="cite_ref-452"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-452">[452]</a></sup>

The Iraqi government remains particularly influenced by Iran, and consults with it on most matters.<sup id="cite_ref-453"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-453">[453]</a></sup> As of 2018 Iran has become Iraq's top trading partner, with an annual turnover of approximately US$12 billion compared to the US$6 billion in trade between Iraq and Saudi Arabia. In addition to fostering economic ties, Tehran furthered its influence by aiding the Iraqi government [in its fight](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2017_Iraqi%E2%80%93Kurdish_conflict "2017 Iraqi–Kurdish conflict") against the [push for independence](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2017_Iraqi_Kurdistan_independence_referendum "2017 Iraqi Kurdistan independence referendum") in [Iraqi Kurdistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan "Iraqi Kurdistan"), which is mainly Sunni.<sup id="cite_ref-454"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-454">[454]</a></sup> Saudi Arabia has responded by strengthening its ties to the [Kurdistan Regional Government](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kurdistan_Regional_Government "Kurdistan Regional Government"), seeing it as a barrier to the expansion of Iranian influence in the region, while also adopting a [soft power](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soft_power "Soft power") approach to improve relations with the Iraqi government.<sup id="cite_ref-Ibish_357-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-Ibish-357">[357]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-economist.com_358-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-economist.com-358">[358]</a></sup>

[Izzat Ibrahim al-Douri](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Izzat_Ibrahim_al-Douri "Izzat Ibrahim al-Douri"), former Ba'athist official and leader of the [Naqshbandi Army](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naqshbandi_Army "Naqshbandi Army") insurgent group, has repeatedly praised Saudi efforts to constrain Iranian clout in Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters_2016-04-10_50-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-Reuters_2016-04-10-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rudaw_51-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-Rudaw-51">[51]</a></sup>

Recently, Saudi Arabia has developed a close relationship with Shia cleric [Muqtada al-Sadr](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muqtada_al-Sadr "Muqtada al-Sadr"), the leader of the [Sadrist Movement](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sadrist_Movement "Sadrist Movement") and the [Peace Companies](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peace_Companies "Peace Companies") militia as well as a critic of both U.S. and Iranian involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-Haddad_455-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-Haddad-455">[455]</a></sup>

### Bahraini uprising [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=47 "Edit section: Bahraini uprising")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:March_of_9_march_7.JPG)

[Bahraini anti-government protests](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aftermath_of_the_Bahraini_uprising_(January%E2%80%93August_2012) "Aftermath of the Bahraini uprising (January–August 2012)") in 2012

Saudi Arabia and Iran have sought to extend their influence in Bahrain for decades. While the majority of Muslims in Bahrain are Shia, the country is ruled by the Sunni [Al Khalifa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Khalifa "House of Khalifa") family – who are widely viewed as being subservient to the Saudi government. Iran claimed sovereignty over Bahrain until 1970, when Shah [Mohammad Reza Pahlavi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Pahlavi "Mohammad Reza Pahlavi") abandoned claims after negotiations with the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-rhm_456-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-rhm-456">[456]</a></sup> The Iranian Revolution led to resumed interest in Bahraini affairs. In 1981, the front organization [Islamic Front for the Liberation of Bahrain](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_Front_for_the_Liberation_of_Bahrain "Islamic Front for the Liberation of Bahrain") led a [failed coup attempt](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1981_Bahraini_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt "1981 Bahraini coup d'état attempt") to install a Shia theocratic regime led by [Hadi al-Modarresi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadi_al-Modarresi "Hadi al-Modarresi"). Since then, the government has accused Iran of supporting terrorist plots within its borders.<sup id="cite_ref-457"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-457">[457]</a></sup>

Sunni states have long feared that Iran might stir up unrest among regional Shia minority populations, especially in Bahrain. [Bahrain government](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politics_of_Bahrain "Politics of Bahrain")'s stability depends heavily on Saudi support. The island is connected to Saudi Arabia by the 25 kilometer [King Fahd Causeway](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/King_Fahd_Causeway "King Fahd Causeway"), and its proximity to Saudi Arabia's oil-rich, the Saudi Shia minority in [Eastern Province](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Province,_Saudi_Arabia "Eastern Province, Saudi Arabia") is viewed by Riyadh as a security concern. Any political gains by the Shia in Bahrain are seen by the Saudis as gains for Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-458"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-458">[458]</a></sup>

In response to the Arab Spring in 2011, the GCC governments sought to maintain their power through social reform, economic handouts, and violent repression. Member states also distributed a share of their combined oil wealth to Bahrain and Oman to maintain stability. Saudi-led GCC forces quickly [intervened](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peninsula_Shield_Force#Role_in_Bahraini_uprising "Peninsula Shield Force") in support of the government of Bahraini to put down the [anti-government uprising](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bahraini_protests_of_2011 "Bahraini protests of 2011") in Bahrain.

The Bahraini government publicly blamed Iran for the protests, but an [independent commission](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bahrain_Independent_Commission_of_Inquiry "Bahrain Independent Commission of Inquiry") established by [King Hamad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hamad_bin_Isa_Al_Khalifa "Hamad bin Isa Al Khalifa") rejected the claim, instead highlighting human rights abuses committed in the crackdown.<sup id="cite_ref-459"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-459">[459]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PenForce_NYT14March_93-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-PenForce_NYT14March-93">[93]</a></sup> The protests, along with the Iran nuclear deal, strained Bahrain's relationship with the United States. Bahrain has sought closer ties with Russia as a result, but this has been limited due to Saudi Arabia's alliance with the US.<sup id="cite_ref-460"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-460">[460]</a></sup>

Following the onset of the Arab Winter, Bahrain accused Iran of orchestrating several domestic incidents as part of a campaign to destabilize the country. Tehran denied all allegations and accused the government of Bahrain of blaming its own internal problems on Iran after every incident.<sup id="cite_ref-DW_November_2017_203-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-DW_November_2017-203">[203]</a></sup> In August 2015, authorities in Bahrain arrested five suspects over a bombing in [Sitra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sitra "Sitra"). Officials linked the attacks to the Revolutionary Guard and [Hezbollah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hezbollah "Hezbollah"), although Iran denied any involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-461"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-461">[461]</a></sup> In January 2016, Bahrain joined Saudi Arabia in cutting diplomatic ties with Tehran following the attacks on Saudi diplomatic missions in Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-462"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-462">[462]</a></sup> In November 2017, Bahrain called an explosion on its main oil pipeline "terrorist sabotage" linked to Iran, drawing a rebuke from Tehran. Saudi Arabia also referred to the incident as an "attack on the pipeline".<sup id="cite_ref-DW_November_2017_203-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-DW_November_2017-203">[203]</a></sup>

### Lebanese politics [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=48 "Edit section: Lebanese politics")

In 2008, Saudi Arabia proposed creating an Arab force backed by US and NATO air and sea power to intervene in Lebanon and destroy Iranian-backed Hezbollah, according to a [leaked US diplomatic cable](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_diplomatic_cables_leak "United States diplomatic cables leak"). According to the cable Saudi argued that a Hezbollah victory against the [Siniora](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fouad_Siniora "Fouad Siniora") government "combined with Iranian actions in Iraq and on the Palestinian front would be a disaster for the US and the entire region".

In February 2016 Saudi Arabia banned their citizens from visiting Lebanon and suspended [military aid](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_aid "Military aid") due to possible Iranian influence and Lebanon's refusal to condemn the attack on Saudi embassy.<sup id="cite_ref-463"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-463">[463]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-464"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-464">[464]</a></sup> Furthermore, Bahrain, Kuwait, and the United Arab Emirates advised all their citizens not to travel to Lebanon and urged them to leave immediately.<sup id="cite_ref-465"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-465">[465]</a></sup>

Lebanese [Prime Minister](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_Lebanon "List of Prime Ministers of Lebanon") [Saad Hariri](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saad_Hariri "Saad Hariri") resigned on 4 November 2017. The situation was seen as a power play by Saudi Arabia to increase its influence in Lebanon and counterbalance Iran's victories in Iraq and Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-466"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-466">[466]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-467"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-467">[467]</a></sup> In a televised speech from Saudi Arabia, Hariri criticized Hezbollah and blamed Iran for causing "disorder and destruction" in Lebanon. Hezbollah leader [Hassan Nasrallah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hassan_Nasrallah "Hassan Nasrallah") responded by accusing Hariri of resigning on Riyadh's orders.<sup id="cite_ref-468"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-468">[468]</a></sup>

### War in Afghanistan [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=49 "Edit section: War in Afghanistan")

The rivalry has contributed to the [ongoing instability](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan_conflict_(1978%E2%80%93present) "Afghanistan conflict (1978–present)") in [Afghanistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan "Afghanistan"). Afghanistan shares ties with Iran through the Persian language, but is strategically important to Saudi Arabia. After the Cold War, Saudi policy shifted from fighting the spread of communism to containing Iranian influence in South and Central Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-afghanistan_111-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-afghanistan-111">[111]</a></sup>

Saudi Arabia was one of three countries to officially recognize the Sunni [Taliban](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taliban "Taliban") government in 1996, along with its allies Pakistan and the United Arab Emirates. During the [Afghan Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1996%E2%80%932001) "Afghan Civil War (1996–2001)"), Iran and Saudi Arabia supported opposing militant factions. Iran assisted the Shia [Hezb-e Wahdat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hezbe_Wahdat "Hezbe Wahdat"), while Saudi Arabia provided financial support to the Wahhabist [Ittihad-e Islami](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_Dawah_Organisation_of_Afghanistan "Islamic Dawah Organisation of Afghanistan").<sup id="cite_ref-469"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-469">[469]</a></sup>

In 2001, the [invasion of Afghanistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_invasion_of_Afghanistan "United States invasion of Afghanistan") and the removal of the Taliban in the wake of the [September 11 attacks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/September_11_attacks "September 11 attacks") benefited Iran, which had previously been on the brink of war with the group. The regime change removed Iran's primary threat along its eastern borders, and the removal of Saddam Hussein two years later further bolstered its position, allowing it to refocus its efforts on other areas, especially Syria and Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-470"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-470">[470]</a></sup> In the ensuing years, Iran sought to expand its influence over Afghanistan. It provided limited support to the Taliban as a potential means of increasing leverage with the Afghan central government and creating a deterrent to conflict with the United States, although the support waned amid growing backlash in Afghanistan against perceived Iranian interference.<sup id="cite_ref-rand_2014_471-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-rand_2014-471">[471]</a></sup> Iran has also sought to expand soft influence by building pro-Iranian schools, mosques, and media centers, and by maintaining close ties with Afghanistan's [Tajik](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tajiks "Tajiks") and [Hazara](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hazaras "Hazaras") populations.<sup id="cite_ref-rand_2014_471-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-rand_2014-471">[471]</a></sup> Originally Iran support the central government against the Taliban, which was still funded by Saudi Arabia in the months following 9/11.<sup id="cite_ref-472"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-472">[472]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-473"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-473">[473]</a></sup> However, due to [American presence](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%932021) "War in Afghanistan (2001–2021)"), Iran resorted to funding the Taliban,<sup id="cite_ref-474"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-474">[474]</a></sup> while Saudi Arabia helped the Afghan Government in reconstruction.<sup id="cite_ref-475"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-475">[475]</a></sup>

### Pakistani sectarian violence [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=50 "Edit section: Pakistani sectarian violence")

Since the 1980s, Pakistan has been dealing with sporadic [sectarian conflict](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sectarian_violence_in_Pakistan "Sectarian violence in Pakistan"), and the Muslim population is predominantly Sunni with about 10–15% Shia adherents.<sup id="cite_ref-476"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-476">[476]</a></sup> The Saudi Arabia enjoys a strong public support from the country's [conservative sphere](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservatism_in_Pakistan "Conservatism in Pakistan") and has occupied a unique statue in Pakistan's foreign policy stature.

Pakistan is economically dependent on oil imports from Saudi Arabia, a key strategic ally but shares some historical cultural ties with Iran. The [foreign employees](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_workers_in_Saudi_Arabia "Foreign workers in Saudi Arabia") Saudi oil industry from Pakistan plays a crucial role in Pakistan's economic stability who sends [large remittances back home](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Remittances_to_Pakistan "Remittances to Pakistan"). The largest amount comes from the 1.5 million Pakistanis working in Saudi Arabia who sent home about US$5.5 billion in remittances in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_22-02-2018_477-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-WSJ_22-02-2018-477">[477]</a></sup> There are also allegations of Saudi Arabia's financial grants to Pakistan's national laboratories that built Pakistan's nuclear weapons program.<sup id="cite_ref-478"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-478">[478]</a></sup> The Saudi monarchy also views the [Balochistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balochistan,_Pakistan "Balochistan, Pakistan") province of Pakistan as a potential means of stirring ethnic unrest in neighboring Iran, with its province of [Sistan and Baluchestan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sistan_and_Baluchestan "Sistan and Baluchestan").<sup id="cite_ref-479"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-479">[479]</a></sup>

In February 2018, Saudi Arabia, acting on behalf of the GCC, joined China and Turkey in opposing a US-led initiative to place Pakistan on an international terror-financing watch list through the [Financial Action Task Force](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Financial_Action_Task_Force_on_Money_Laundering "Financial Action Task Force on Money Laundering"). This move came days after Prime Minister Imran Khan went onto to deploy ~1,000 military troops to the Gulf kingdoms for what it described as an "advisory mission".<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_22-02-2018_477-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-WSJ_22-02-2018-477">[477]</a></sup>

At home, the Pakistani lawmakers have been levelling accusations at Iran of influencing Pakistani Shias to act as proxies to further Iranian interests in Pakistan. The Iranian government has been suspected of militarizing Shias amongst Pakistan's local population and promoting sectarian sentiments to further achieve its goals.<sup id="cite_ref-480"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-480">[480]</a></sup> According to the Pakistani intelligence assessments, many Pakistani Shias have also been suspected of traveling to parts of the Middle East including Syria and Lebanon to fight on behalf of the Iranian government.<sup id="cite_ref-481"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-481">[481]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-482"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-482">[482]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-483"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-483">[483]</a></sup>

### Nigerian Sectarianism [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=51 "Edit section: Nigerian Sectarianism")

The [Islamic Movement of Nigeria](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_Movement_of_Nigeria "Islamic Movement of Nigeria") (IMN), a Shiite religious and political organisation founded and headed by [Sheikh Ibrahim Zakzaki](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibrahim_Zakzaky "Ibrahim Zakzaky"), constitutes another proxy in the conflict. It constitutes another asset that could enable Iran in disrupting Sunni States in West Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Bahi_2018_32_276-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-Bahi_2018_32-276">[276]</a></sup> Thus, Saudi Arabia by supporting the Nigerian government in its suppressing efforts, and by providing support to rival Sunni groups such as [Izala](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Izala_Society "Izala Society") and not seriously condemning Boko Haram's actions, aims to limit its influence and growth in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-Bahi_2018_33_19-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-Bahi_2018_33-19">[19]</a></sup>

### Israeli–Palestinian conflict [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=52 "Edit section: Israeli–Palestinian conflict")

Both Iran and Saudi Arabia have employed different methods to resolve the [Israeli–Palestinian conflict](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict "Israeli–Palestinian conflict"). Iran has preferred a confrontational approach, arming and financing proxies such as [Hamas](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hamas "Hamas") and [Hezbollah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hezbollah "Hezbollah") (and lesser proxies such as [PIJ](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_Jihad_Movement_in_Palestine "Islamic Jihad Movement in Palestine") and [PFLP](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Popular_Front_for_the_Liberation_of_Palestine "Popular Front for the Liberation of Palestine")) in its ongoing [proxy conflict](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Israel_proxy_conflict "Iran–Israel proxy conflict") with [Israel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel "Israel"). Saudi Arabia has taken a diplomatic approach, such as backing [Fatah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fatah "Fatah")'s negotiations with Israel and the [Arab Peace Initiative](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Peace_Initiative "Arab Peace Initiative") announced in 2002 and re-endorsed in 2007 and 2013 to bring about a comprehensive peace accord with Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-484"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-484">[484]</a></sup> Saudi Arabia strengthened ties with Israel in 2018 when Crown Prince [Mohammad bin Salman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammad_bin_Salman "Mohammad bin Salman") stated that Israelis have a right to their own land.<sup id="cite_ref-485"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-485">[485]</a></sup>

### Libyan Crisis [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=53 "Edit section: Libyan Crisis")

Iran and Saudi Arabia have waged a proxy war in [Libya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya "Libya"), with Saudi Arabia,<sup id="cite_ref-486"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-486">[486]</a></sup> along with the U.A.E,<sup id="cite_ref-487"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-487">[487]</a></sup> Egypt, and Sudan, have provided support to the [Libyan National Army](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_National_Army "Libyan National Army"), and its leader [warlord](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warlord "Warlord") [Khalifa Haftar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khalifa_Haftar "Khalifa Haftar"). Iran, Qatar, and Turkey support the [Government of National Accord](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_National_Accord "Government of National Accord") and other Islamist forces in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-488"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-488">[488]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-489"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-489">[489]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-490"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-490">[490]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-491"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-491">[491]</a></sup> According to the UN, Iran also sent weapons to the GNA and its affiliates.<sup id="cite_ref-492"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-492">[492]</a></sup>

## Nuclear programs of Iran and Saudi Arabia [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=54 "Edit section: Nuclear programs of Iran and Saudi Arabia")

Although both Iran and Saudi Arabia signed the [Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Non-Proliferation_of_Nuclear_Weapons "Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons") in 1970 and 1988 respectively, a potential nuclear arms race has been a concern for years. Both governments claim that their programs are for peaceful purposes, but foreign governments and organizations have accused both of taking steps to obtain nuclear weapons capabilities.

Iran's ongoing [nuclear program](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_program_of_Iran "Nuclear program of Iran") began in the 1950s under the Shah in cooperation with the United States as part of the [Atoms for Peace](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atoms_for_Peace "Atoms for Peace") program. The cooperation continued until the Iranian Revolution in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-493"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-493">[493]</a></sup> [Sanctions](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanctions_on_Iran "Sanctions on Iran") have been in place since then, and were expanded in 2006 with the passage of United Nations Security Council [Resolution 1737](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1737 "United Nations Security Council Resolution 1737") and [Resolution 1696](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1696 "United Nations Security Council Resolution 1696") in response to Iran's uranium enrichment program.

Saudi Arabia has considered several options in response to the Iranian program: acquiring its own [nuclear capability](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_program_of_Saudi_Arabia "Nuclear program of Saudi Arabia") as a deterrent, entering into an alliance with an existing nuclear power, or pursuing a regional [nuclear-weapon-free zone](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear-weapon-free_zone "Nuclear-weapon-free zone") agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-494"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-494">[494]</a></sup> It is believed that Saudi Arabia has been a major financier of Pakistan's integrated [nuclear program](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction "Pakistan and weapons of mass destruction") since 1974, a project begun under former [Prime Minister](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Pakistan "Prime Minister of Pakistan") [Zulfikar Ali Bhutto](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto "Zulfikar Ali Bhutto"). In 2003 it was reported that Saudi Arabia had taken the "strategic decision" to acquire "off-the-shelf" atomic weapons from Pakistan, according to senior American officials.<sup id="cite_ref-495"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-495">[495]</a></sup> In 2003, _[The Washington Times](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Washington_Times "The Washington Times")_ reported that Pakistan and Saudi Arabia had entered a secret agreement on nuclear cooperation to provide the Saudis with nuclear weapons technology in return for access to cheap oil for Pakistan.<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>

Following several years of negotiations for a [nuclear deal framework](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran_nuclear_deal_framework "Iran nuclear deal framework") between Iran and the [P5+1](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/P5%2B1 "P5+1") countries, the [Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joint_Comprehensive_Plan_of_Action "Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action") (JCPOA) was signed in 2015. The deal raised concerns for Saudi Arabia, which saw it as a step toward reducing Iran's international isolation and potentially exacerbating the proxy conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-496"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-496">[496]</a></sup> However, Riyadh did not publicly denounce the deal at the time as Israel did.<sup id="cite_ref-497"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-497">[497]</a></sup> In 2018, Crown Prince Mohammad bin Salman stated that Saudi Arabia would move to obtain nuclear weapons if Iran's program is successful.<sup id="cite_ref-498"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-498">[498]</a></sup> He led a delegation to the United States to meet with [Trump administration](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidency_of_Donald_Trump "Presidency of Donald Trump") officials to discuss mutual concerns, including a potential US withdrawal from the Iran nuclear agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-CNBC_19-03-18_212-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-CNBC_19-03-18-212">[212]</a></sup> In April 2018, Israeli [Prime Minister](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Israel "Prime Minister of Israel") [Benjamin Netanyahu](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Netanyahu "Benjamin Netanyahu") gave a televised speech accusing Iran of covertly continuing the [AMAD Project](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/AMAD_Project "AMAD Project") in violation of the JCPOA.<sup id="cite_ref-499"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-499">[499]</a></sup>

[President](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_the_United_States "President of the United States") Trump announced on 8 May 2018 that the United States would [withdraw](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_withdrawal_from_the_Joint_Comprehensive_Plan_of_Action "United States withdrawal from the Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action") from the JCPOA and reinstate previous [sanctions against Iran](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanctions_against_Iran "Sanctions against Iran") in addition to imposing new sanctions.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt_08-05-18_214-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-nyt_08-05-18-214">[214]</a></sup> In anticipation of the decision, Iranian [President](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_Iran "President of Iran") [Rouhani](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hassan_Rouhani "Hassan Rouhani") stated that Iran would remain in the deal if the remaining parties did the same, but was otherwise vague on how the country would respond to the US decision.<sup id="cite_ref-500"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-500">[500]</a></sup>

## Iran–Saudi Arabia rapprochement [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=55 "Edit section: Iran–Saudi Arabia rapprochement")

### Earlier negotiations [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=56 "Edit section: Earlier negotiations")

Since April 2021, [Iraq](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraq "Iraq") has hosted five rounds of direct talks between the two nations, which cut diplomatic ties in 2016. The 6th round of talks on a ministerial level stalled, but after a meeting in [Amman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amman "Amman"), [Jordan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jordan "Jordan"), in December 2022, Iran's [Hossein Amirabdollahian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hossein_Amirabdollahian "Hossein Amirabdollahian") and Saudi Foreign Minister Prince [Faisal bin Farhan Al Saud](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faisal_bin_Farhan_Al_Saud "Faisal bin Farhan Al Saud") signaled that both countries would be "open to more dialogue". Despite increased rhetoric by Iran against Saudi Arabia, the Saudis support efforts to revive the stalled [Iran nuclear deal](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/JCPOA "JCPOA"). However, the Saudi minister threatened to nullify the negotiations and following agreement if Iran were to obtain nuclear weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-501"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-501">[501]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-502"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-502">[502]</a></sup> In January 2023, the Saudi foreign ministers Al Saud speaking at a panel at the [World Economic Forum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Economic_Forum "World Economic Forum") in [Davos](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Davos "Davos") reiterated that "Riyadh is trying to find a dialog with Iran". He welcomed the decision by Saudi Arabia and other [Gulf states](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_states_of_the_Persian_Gulf "Arab states of the Persian Gulf") to focus on their economies "towards joint prosperity".<sup id="cite_ref-503"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-503">[503]</a></sup>

### March 2023 restoration of relations [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict&action=edit&section=57 "Edit section: March 2023 restoration of relations")

The two countries announced the resumption of relations on 10 March 2023, following a deal brokered by China.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_504-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-:1-504">[504]</a></sup> The agreement was signed by secretary of the [Supreme National Security Council of Iran](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supreme_National_Security_Council "Supreme National Security Council") [Ali Shamkhahni](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ali_Shamkhani "Ali Shamkhani") and Saudi Arabian national security advisor [Musaed Al-Aiban](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Musaad_bin_Mohammed_Al_Aiban "Musaad bin Mohammed Al Aiban").<sup id="cite_ref-505"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-505">[505]</a></sup> A joint statement announced that their embassies would re-open within two months.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_504-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-:1-504">[504]</a></sup> The [United Nations Secretary General](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Secretary_General "United Nations Secretary General"), [Hezbollah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hezbollah "Hezbollah"), [Oman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oman "Oman"), [UAE](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/UAE "UAE"), [Egypt](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egypt "Egypt"),<sup id="cite_ref-506"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-506">[506]</a></sup> [Pakistan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan "Pakistan"),<sup id="cite_ref-507"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-507">[507]</a></sup> [Indonesia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indonesia "Indonesia")<sup id="cite_ref-508"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-508">[508]</a></sup> and [Malaysia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malaysia "Malaysia")<sup id="cite_ref-509"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-509">[509]</a></sup> welcomed the reapproachment, while two former Israeli Prime Ministers criticized it.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_510-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-guardian-510">[510]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-511"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-511">[511]</a></sup>

Under the deal, the countries would restore agreements related to security, trade, and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-2023resume_512-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-2023resume-512">[512]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_Saudi_Arabia_and_Iran_Agree_to_Restore_Ties_513-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-nytimes_Saudi_Arabia_and_Iran_Agree_to_Restore_Ties-513">[513]</a></sup> Under the agreement both countries agreed to respect the other's sovereignty and not interfere in the internal affairs of the other.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_510-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-guardian-510">[510]</a></sup> The rapprochement deal was concluded after [Ali Khamenei's](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ali_Khamenei "Ali Khamenei") offer to end Iran's military support to [Houthi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Houthi_movement "Houthi movement") militants in Yemen and abide by the [UN arms embargo](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arms_embargo "Arms embargo"), an agreement that pressurizes the Houthis towards a negotiated settlement with the [Yemeni government](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cabinet_of_Yemen "Cabinet of Yemen"). Saudi and US officials remarked that subsequent Iranian commitments towards de-escalating [Yemeni conflict](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yemeni_civil_war_(2014%E2%80%93present) "Yemeni civil war (2014–present)") were a "litmus test" for the survival of [Chinese](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China")\-brokered reconciliation deal.<sup id="cite_ref-Nissenbaum,_Said,_Falcon_123-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-Nissenbaum,_Said,_Falcon-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-514"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-514">[514]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arab_News_124-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-Arab_News-124">[124]</a></sup>

On 20 March 2023, [Iranian President](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iranian_President "Iranian President") [Ebrahim Raisi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ebrahim_Raisi "Ebrahim Raisi") has "welcomed" an invitation from Saudi Arabia's [King Salman](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/King_Salman "King Salman") to visit the kingdom, according to _France24_. Earlier Iran's top security official [Ali Shamkhani](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ali_Shamkhani "Ali Shamkhani") also held talks with Emirati President [Mohamed bin Zayed Al Nahyan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohamed_bin_Zayed_Al_Nahyan "Mohamed bin Zayed Al Nahyan") in [Abu Dhabi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abu_Dhabi "Abu Dhabi"), which paved the way for more security in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-515"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_note-515">[515]</a></sup>

"The Safavis also institutionalised what was to be another central defining difference between Arabs and Persians, the predominance of Shi'ite Islam in Iran. This made formal the religious difference between Arabs and Persians that had been smouldering since the early years of Islam. In subsequent nationalist rhetoric the Iranians could be seen as shu'ûbiyyin, defectors from both Arabism and the orthodox faith, while in Khomeini's rhetoric Saddam was associated with Yazid, the Umayyad tyrant who killed Hussain at Karbala in 680AD"

431.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_ref-431 "Jump up")** ["Pakistan: Threat of Saudi-Iran War Subsiding Due to Diplomacy by Islamabad"](https://www.voanews.com/middle-east/pakistan-threat-saudi-iran-war-subsiding-due-diplomacy-islamabad). Voice of America. 16 October 2019. But with its large Shi'ite minority and a nearly 900-kilometer (560 mile) border with Iran, Islamabad has largely stayed neutral in Middle East tensions. It declined a Saudi call a few years back to join the military alliance fighting the Houthi insurgents in Yemen, winning praise from Iran.
432.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict#cite_ref-432 "Jump up")** ["Why Pakistan Isn't Taking Sides After Outbreak of US-Iran Conflict"](https://www.voanews.com/middle-east/voa-news-iran/why-pakistan-isnt-taking-sides-after-outbreak-us-iran-conflict). Voice of America. 25 January 2020. More recently, then-Pakistani Prime Minister Nawaz Sharif first declared neutrality in Yemen's ongoing conflict between a Saudi-led coalition and Iranian-backed Houthi militants in 2015. Sharif's successor, Prime Minister Khan, has continued that approach.
